[<p>[BENGALURU] UK shares ended a wobbly session in the red as weak US factory data piled on worries over the world's economy that were triggered by smartphone giant Apple's rare revenue cut, taking the shine off a positive Christmas update from retailer Next.</p>, <p>The FTSE 100 ended 0.6 per cent lower and the mid-cap index fell 0.8 per cent.</p>, <p>Both indexes saw a small comeback around noon when pharmaceutical companies boosted the main index higher, after Bristol-Myers Squibb's US$74 billion purchase of Celgene.</p>, <p>Pharma giants AstraZeneca and GlaxoSmithKline recouped earlier losses and rose 1.8 per cent and 0.4 per cent respectively, while a resurgence in crude prices helped oil majors eke out gains.</p>, <p></p>, <p>But Apple's comments on "economic deceleration" in Greater China plus data showing that US factory activity slowed more than expected in December ultimately had the final word.<div class="view view-more view-id-more view-display-id-related_articles related-articles view-dom-id-46a8f533e462647521e34be3e3889077">\n<div class="view-content">\n<div class="views-row views-row-1 views-row-odd views-row-first views-row-last">\n<div> <span><a href="/stocks/europe-shares-start-2019-shakily-as-china-eu-figures-deepen-gloom"><span>SEE ALSO: </span>Europe: Shares start 2019 shakily as China, EU figures deepen gloom</a></span> </div> </div>\n</div>\n</div><p><div id="innity-in-post"></div><div class="dfp-tag-wrapper" id="dfp-ad-midarticlespecial-wrapper">\n<div class="dfp-tag-wrapper" id="dfp-ad-midarticlespecial">\n<script type="text/javascript">\n    googletag.cmd.push(function() {\n      googletag.display("dfp-ad-midarticlespecial");\n    });\n  </script>\n</div>\n</div><p></p>\n<p>Investors dumped stocks sensitive to China, the world's second-largest economy, and took refuge in gold, seen as a safe haven.</p>\n<p>"What the market is wrestling with is whether it is indicative of a wider malaise in perhaps both the world economy and China," said Peter Rutter, head of global equities at Royal London Asset Management.</p>\n<p>HSBC edged 0.8 per cent lower and Standard Chartered lost 3.2 per cent, while luxury brand Burberry, also sensitive to signs of slowing demand in China, lost 6 per cent to join the top fallers.</p>\n<p>Concerns over economic growth in top metals consumer China sent Rio Tinto, BHP, Glencore and Antofagasta down between 1.3 and 4.8 per cent.</p>\n<p>Blue-chip insurer Hiscox dipped nearly 5 per cent after hacker/hacking group "The Dark Overlord" claimed to have stolen 18,000 files from UK insurance firms involved in litigation for the 9/11 attacks in New York.</p>\n<p>A MIXED BAG OF NEWS</p>\n<p>One bright spot helping contain negative sentiment was high street clothing retailer Next, which added 4.1 per cent after reporting higher sales in the Christmas period thanks to a late surge in online demand.</p>\n<p>"November was indeed difficult for Next as well, but Christmas did arrive ultimately, with the last three weeks of December being very strong in sales terms," said Peel Hunt analysts, while Investec called it a respectable trading update.</p>\n<p>Next's encouraging update also helped shares in Marks &amp; Spencer, Tesco, WM Morrison and Primark-owner Associated British Foods rise between 1.3 and 4.1 per cent, among top blue-chip winners.</p>\n<p>Prominent mid-cap and small-cap retailers, including Superdry, Dunelm, Debenhams, rose. AIM-listed ASOS was up 5.7 per cent, also boosted by Peel Hunt reinstating a "buy" rating on the online fashion store a month after its profit alert shook the global retail scene.</p>\n<p>Still, investors continued to fret about the US-China trade spat, a slowdown in the global economy, Brexit uncertainties, plunging oil prices - to name but a few.</p>\n<p>Data showing growth in Britain's construction sector fell to a three-month low in December did little to help the mood, highlighting delays in commercial projects due to Britain's impending departure from the European Union.</p>\n<p>Jitters over how Britain would end up leaving the European Union has also restricted spending by companies and consumers alike, as a mid-January parliamentary vote on Prime Minister Theresa May's disputed EU divorce deal looms.</p>\n<p>Brexit minister Stephen Barclay said Britain was more likely to end up leaving the European Union without a deal if parliament rejects May's agreement.</p>\n<p>Bank of England Governor Mark Carney warned in November that a "no deal" scenario would be an economic shock akin to the 1970s oil crisis.</p>\n<p>Industrial and bank shares were among the top drags on the mid-cap index, and only consumer staples and real estate stocks ended in black.</p>\n<p>Among small-caps, drugmaker Vectura soared 13 per cent to lead the gainers after a positive trading update and AIM-listed Faroe Petroleum rose 4.9 per cent after DNO's takeover offer became mandatory.</p>\n<p>REUTERS</p>\n</p></p>, <p><div id="innity-in-post"></div><div class="dfp-tag-wrapper" id="dfp-ad-midarticlespecial-wrapper">\n<div class="dfp-tag-wrapper" id="dfp-ad-midarticlespecial">\n<script type="text/javascript">\n    googletag.cmd.push(function() {\n seen as a safe haven.</p>\n<p>"What the market is wrestling with is whether it is indicative of a wider malaise in perhaps both the world economy and China," said Peter Rutter, head of global equities at Royal London Asset Management.</p>\n<p>HSBC edged 0.8 per cent lower and Standard Chartered lost 3.2 per cent, while luxury brand Burberry, also sensitive to signs of slowing demand in China, lost 6 per cent to join the top fallers.</p>\n<p>Concerns over economic growth in top metals consumer China sent Rio Tinto, BHP, Glencore and Antofagasta down between 1.3 and 4.8 per cent.</p>\n<p>Blue-chip insurer Hiscox dipped nearly 5 per cent after hacker/hacking group "The Dark Overlord" claimed to have stolen 18,000 files from UK insurance firms involved in litigation for the 9/11 attacks in New York.</p>\n<p>A MIXED BAG OF NEWS</p>\n<p>One bright spot helping contain negative sentiment was high street clothing retailer Next, which added 4.1 per cent after reporting higher sales in the Christmas period thanks to a late surge in online demand.</p>\n<p>"November was indeed difficult for Next as well, but Christmas did arrive ultimately, with the last three weeks of December being very strong in sales terms," said Peel Hunt analysts, while Investec called it a respectable trading update.</p>\n<p>Next's encouraging update also helped shares in Marks &amp; Spencer, Tesco, WM Morrison and Primark-owner Associated British Foods rise between 1.3 and 4.1 per cent, among top blue-chip winners.</p>\n<p>Prominent mid-cap and small-cap retailers, including Superdry, Dunelm, Debenhams, rose. AIM-listed ASOS was up 5.7 per cent, also boosted by Peel Hunt reinstating a "buy" rating on the online fashion store a month after its profit alert shook the global retail scene.</p>\n<p>Still, investors continued to fret about the US-China trade spat, a slowdown in the global economy, Brexit uncertainties, plunging oil prices - to name but a few.</p>\n<p>Data showing growth in Britain's construction sector fell to a three-month low in December did little to help the mood, highlighting delays in commercial projects due to Britain's impending departure from the European Union.</p>\n<p>Jitters over how Britain would end up leaving the European Union has also restricted spending by companies and consumers alike, as a mid-January parliamentary vote on Prime Minister Theresa May's disputed EU divorce deal looms.</p>\n<p>Brexit minister Stephen Barclay said Britain was more likely to end up leaving the European Union without a deal if parliament rejects May's agreement.</p>\n<p>Bank of England Governor Mark Carney warned in November that a "no deal" scenario would be an economic shock akin to the 1970s oil crisis.</p>\n<p>Industrial and bank shares were among the top drags on the mid-cap index, and only consumer staples and real estate stocks ended in black.</p>\n<p>Among small-caps, drugmaker Vectura soared 13 per cent to lead the gainers after a positive trading update and AIM-listed Faroe Petroleum rose 4.9 per cent after DNO's takeover offer became mandatory.</p>\n<p>REUTERS</p>\n</p>, <p></p>, <p>Investors dumped stocks sensitive to China, the world's second-largest economy, and took refuge in gold, seen as a safe haven.</p>, <p>"What the market is wrestling with is whether it is indicative of a wider malaise in perhaps both the world economy and China," said Peter Rutter, head of global equities at Royal London Asset Management.</p>, <p>HSBC edged 0.8 per cent lower and Standard Chartered lost 3.2 per cent, while luxury brand Burberry, also sensitive to signs of slowing demand in China, lost 6 per cent to join the top fallers.</p>, <p>Concerns over economic growth in top metals consumer China sent Rio Tinto, BHP, Glencore and Antofagasta down between 1.3 and 4.8 per cent.</p>, <p>Blue-chip insurer Hiscox dipped nearly 5 per cent after hacker/hacking group "The Dark Overlord" claimed to have stolen 18,000 files from UK insurance firms involved in litigation for the 9/11 attacks in New York.</p>, <p>A MIXED BAG OF NEWS</p>, <p>One bright spot helping contain negative sentiment was high street clothing retailer Next, which added 4.1 per cent after reporting higher sales in the Christmas period thanks to a late surge in online demand.</p>, <p>"November was indeed difficult for Next as well, but Christmas did arrive ultimately, with the last three weeks of December being very strong in sales terms," said Peel Hunt analysts, while Investec called it a respectable trading update.</p>, <p>Next's encouraging update also helped shares in Marks &amp; Spencer, Tesco, WM Morrison and Primark-owner Associated British Foods rise between 1.3 and 4.1 per cent, among top blue-chip winners.</p>, <p>Prominent mid-cap and small-cap retailers, including Superdry, Dunelm, Debenhams, rose. AIM-listed ASOS was up 5.7 per cent, also boosted by Peel Hunt reinstating a "buy" rating on the online fashion store a month after its profit alert shook the global retail scene.</p>, <p>Still, investors continued to fret about the US-China trade spat, a slowdown in the global economy, Brexit uncertainties, plunging oil prices - to name but a few.</p>, <p>Data showing growth in Britain's construction sector fell to a three-month low in December did little to help the mood, highlighting delays in commercial projects due to Britain's impending departure from the European Union.</p>, <p>Jitters over how Britain would end up leaving the European Union has also restricted spending by companies and consumers alike, as a mid-January parliamentary vote on Prime Minister Theresa May's disputed EU divorce deal looms.</p>, <p>Brexit minister Stephen Barclay said Britain was more likely to end up leaving the European Union without a deal if parliament rejects May's agreement.</p>, <p>Bank of England Governor Mark Carney warned in November that a "no deal" scenario would be an economic shock akin to the 1970s oil crisis.</p>, <p>Industrial and bank shares were among the top drags on the mid-cap index, and only consumer staples and real estate stocks ended in black.</p>, <p>Among small-caps, drugmaker Vectura soared 13 per cent to lead the gainers after a positive trading update and AIM-listed Faroe Petroleum rose 4.9 per cent after DNO's takeover offer became mandatory.</p>, <p>REUTERS</p>, <p class="text-center">SPH Digital News</p>, <p class="text-center">\xa9 2019 SINGAPORE PRESS HOLDINGS LTD. [<p>WASHINGTON \u2014 Armchair NFL fans with Verizon FiOS who are hoping to watch Sunday\u2019s Chargers-Ravens wild card game on WUSA/Channel 9 should consider making alternative viewing plans.</p>, <p>Thanks to an ongoing contract dispute, Channel 9 is<a href="https://wtop.com/tv/2019/01/wusa-9-cbs-shows-go-dark-for-verizon-fios-subscribers/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"> still blacked out on FiOS.</a></p>, <p>The money-related programming conflict is between Tegna, which is Channel 9\u2019s parent company, and Verizon, and there\u2019s no sign when it will end. In fact, the dispute appears to be getting worse.</p>, <p>Verizon announced Wednesday afternoon that Tegna was no longer negotiating with them, <a href="https://wtop.com/news/2019/01/wusa-goes-dark-for-verizon-fios-customers/" rel="noopener" target="_blank">the Washington Business Journal reported.</a></p>, <p>One option for Ravens fans with FiOS is to livestream the game through a provider such as fuboTV, Hulu Plus, or YouTube TV.</p>, <p>\u201cThere\u2019s a bunch of them out there, and most of them give you a seven day trial period,\u201d said Ken Colburn of Data Doctors.</p>, <p>For example,\xa0CBS All Access offers a one-week free trial and Hulu offers streaming from top sports networks like CBS and live NFL and NCAA football <a href="https://www.hulu.com/live-sports?&amp;cmp=8659&amp;utm_source=google&amp;utm_medium=cpc&amp;utm_campaign=BM%20Search%20Sitelink%20Live&amp;utm_term=+live%20+sports%20+hulu&amp;ds_rl=1244764&amp;ds_rl=1244764&amp;gclid=CjwKCAiAgrfhBRA3EiwAnfF4tm_I3jwSUe45o5e4Bb_s9NXSyR8wFkg0V3yT90Hbch1jgOMbXB1wqxoCzcwQAvD_BwE&amp;gclsrc=aw.ds" rel="noopener" target="_blank">through a free trial\xa0</a>for new subscribers and eligible existing subscribers.</p>, <p>Another idea? Buy an HDTV antenna to pick up Channel 9\u2019s free over-the-air signal.</p>, <p>\u201cFortunately they\u2019re really inexpensive. They\u2019re $20 to $30. There\u2019s lots of them on Amazon. In fact, Amazon itself has an Amazon Basics line of indoor antennas that work really well,\u201d said Colburn. \u201cThe single most important thing to ensure that you get the best possible signal is make sure you get an HDTV antenna that comes with its own amplifier.\u201d</p>, <p>If you live in an urban area you shouldn\u2019t have any problem picking up major local channels, but Colburn said you can plug your address into\xa0<a href="http://tvfool.com/" rel="noopener" target="_blank">TV Fool</a> to find out which direction the broadcast signals are coming from so you can best position your antenna.</p>, <p>The FCC has <a href="https://transition.fcc.gov/cgb/consumerfacts/dtvantennas.pdf" rel="noopener" target="_blank">a helpful guide</a> to antennas and digital TV. Colburn also shared <a href="https://www.datadoctors.com/help/kenscolumns/22229-Wading-Through-Streaming-TV-Options/" rel="noopener" target="_blank">a very useful tool</a> for comparing various streaming services.</p>, <p><em>EDITOR\u2019S NOTE: A previous version of this story incorrectly stated that Amazon Prime members can get a 30-day free trail of CBS All Access.\xa0</em></p>, <p><em>Like WTOP on <a href="https://www.facebook.com/wtopnews">Facebook</a> and follow <a href="https://twitter.com/WTOP">@WTOP</a> on Twitter to engage in conversation about this article and others.</em></p>, <p>\xa9 2019 WTOP. And when we say oversized, we mean\xa0100cm (over 3ft) tall, and roomy enough to comfortably fit a small child\u2014or several.</p>, <p class="a1dbe d0e40">The label behind the bag known as \u201cThe Backpacker\u2019s Closet\u201d specializes in outdoor gear, and bills the item as an ideal pack for camping, traveling, or even as an alternative closet. It reportedly weighs in at about 3.5 lbs and has an additional backstrap and shoulder padding for added comfort:</p>, <p class="a1dbe d0e40">The backpack, which retails for nearly 26,000 yen ($240) on the Japanese retailer Plywood, is <a href="http://www.plywood.jp/shop/shopdetail.html?brandcode=000000006176&amp;search=backpack&amp;sort=">sold out</a> in all three of its sensible colors (beige, olive, and black) and is set to be restocked. Plywood did not immediately respond to a request for comment, so it\u2019s unclear how many of the packs were initially stocked on the site, or whether CWF will actually need to manufacture more to meet demand.</p>, <p class="a1dbe d0e40">While it remains to be seen whether the backpack takes off globally, there are indicators that it has potential. The pack itself <a href="https://nypost.com/2019/01/02/the-new-years-first-horrible-fashion-trend-is-human-sized-backpacks/">is reportedly</a> getting plenty of hype in Japan, and has been shared on Japanese social media by those who have actually snagged one:</p>, <p class="a1dbe d0e40">And it wouldn\u2019t be the first time a gimmicky item\u2019s social media virility translated to actual sales. Last year, the\xa0<a href="https://www.lyst.com/year-in-fashion-2018/">fashion search-engine Lyst</a>\xa0noted that searches for \u201cmeme fashion\u201d saw huge spikes that were driven by viral images of particularly absurd-looking, overlarge, or too-tiny items (see <a href="https://www.jacquemus.com/product/le-grand-chapeau-bomba/">Jacquemus\u2019s Bomba hat</a> or Kendall Jenner <a href="https://twitter.com/VogueParis/status/1054300008760201216/photo/1?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw%7Ctwcamp%5Etweetembed%7Ctwterm%5E1054300008760201216&amp;ref_url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.today.com%2Fstyle%2Fkendall-jenner-s-gigantic-winter-coat-has-internet-cracking-t140721">in an enormous red Aritizia puffer</a>):</p>, <p class="a1dbe d0e40">The \u201cBackpacker\u2019s Closet\u201d may prove to have the same viral potential. And it may portend another year of outlandish fashion.</p>]

[<p>Chipotle, eager to promote itself as a healthy choice, tweaked its menu to fit with trendy diet plans.</p>, <p>Beginning Wednesday, the Mexican food chain will begin offering \u201cLifestyle Bowls,\u201d a new collection of meals that fit into paleo, ketogenic and Whole30 diets.</p>, <p>\u201cThese first-to-category, diet-driven menu offerings are helping those who have committed to living a healthier lifestyle by making it easy to order delicious bowls that only contain the real ingredients permitted by certain diet regimens,\u201d the company said in a statement.</p>, <p>The new bowls can be ordered on Chipotle\u2019s (<a href="https://money.cnn.com/quote/quote.html?symb=CMG">CMG</a>) app or online. The items consist of ingredients already on the menu, so walk-in eaters can also order the bowls.</p>, <p>The diet bowls include:</p>, <p>All three of these diets follow strict rules and <a href="https://money.cnn.com/2018/09/17/news/companies/keto-diet-trend/index.html">are growing in popularity.</a></p>, <p>The ketogenic diet includes low-carb, high-fat foods that force the body into a state of ketosis \xe2\u20ac\u201d when you burn fat, instead of carbohydrates, for energy. Whole30 cuts out sugar, grains, dairy and legumes for 30 days, and paleo is modeled after eating habits in the paleolithic era.</p>, <p>The move toward diet food is another step in Chipotle\u2019s reinvention, as it works to recover from a series of health scares at its restaurants.</p>, <p>The new bowls are latest change under CEO Brian Niccol, the former head of Taco Bell, who <a href="https://www.cnn.com/2018/12/21/investing/chipotle-brian-niccol/index.html">has turned the restaurant around</a> after a number of people got sick during multiple outbreaks of foodborne illness in recent years. The most serious was an E. coli outbreak in 2015 that sickened 60 customers in 14 states.</p>, <p>In 2018 the company closed <a href="https://money.cnn.com/2018/08/16/news/companies/chipotle-retraining-staff/index.html">2,000 locations to retrain staff on food safety</a>.</p>, <p>Chipotle stock skyrocketed 40% last year and is one of the <a href="http://www.cnn.com/2018/12/31/investing/best-stocks-2018/index.html">top-10 performers in the S&amp;P 500</a> since Niccol joined. He also started testing a slew of new items, like milkshakes, beefed up its digital offerings and launched a new advertising campaign.</p>, <p><em>This content was republished with permission from <a href="https://www.cnn.com/" rel="noopener" target="_blank">CNN</a>.</em></p>, <p>Need a break? Play a quick game of solitaire or Sudoku. Or take one of our fun quizzes!</p>]
[<p><input type="checkbox"/>Keep Me Logged In</p>, <p><a class="inline_quotes" data-gdsid="8016" data-inline-quote-symbol="AAPL" href="//www.cnbc.com/quotes/?symbol=AAPL">Apple</a>'s slowdown in China could mean bad news for <a class="inline_quotes" data-gdsid="10848" data-inline-quote-symbol="BBY" href="//www.cnbc.com/quotes/?symbol=BBY">Best Buy</a>, according to one analyst.</p>, <p>The iPhone maker <a href="/2019/01/02/apple-warns-on-q1-results.html">cut its sales forecast Wednesday</a> \u2014 a rare move by the tech giant \u2014 calling out weak sales in China and a trade war that's levied high tariffs over the last several months across hundreds of products and commodities sold between the U.S. and Asia. Apple's stock then <a href="/2019/01/03/apple-stock-falls-after-cutting-q1-guidance-on-weak-iphone-sales.html">cratered as much as 10 percent Thursday</a>, closing with its biggest single-day loss in six years, as investors started to question the broader implications of this announcement.</p>, <p>Electronics retailer Best Buy, being a key vendor partner to Apple, could now face some challenges, UBS analyst Michael Lasser said Thursday in a note to clients.</p>, <p>UBS has estimated that roughly 15 to 20 percent of Best Buy's total sales are of Apple products, which tend to sell at higher price points than other merchandise in Best Buy stores.</p>, <p>And "Apple's significance to Best Buy goes beyond just its direct sales contribution," Lasser said. Apple products "drive traffic and motivate consumers" to go into Best Buy stores in the first place, he said.</p>, <p>As recently as last month, Best Buy has argued its relationship with Apple is still going strong.</p>, <p>"We've built over the years a very unique experience selling Apple products," Best Buy CEO Hubert Joly told analysts on an earnings conference call. "Apple would say ... Best Buy provides the best retail experience for their products and services outside of their own home."</p>, <p>Best Buy has about 900 Apple stores within its stores in addition to roughly 3,000 employees who focus solely on selling Apple devices.</p>, <p>Joly added that "it's unclear at this point what the net effect is going to be" for Best Buy now that <a class="inline_quotes" data-gdsid="9399" data-inline-quote-symbol="AMZN" href="//www.cnbc.com/quotes/?symbol=AMZN">Amazon</a> is starting to sell Apple devices on its website. But this remains a top concern for the retailer on Wall Street, moving forward.</p>, <p>As Apple looks to sell its iPhones and other devices through <a class="inline_quotes" data-gdsid="13906" data-inline-quote-symbol="COST" href="//www.cnbc.com/quotes/?symbol=COST">Costco</a>, Amazon and other distributors to boost sales growth, it will make it harder for Best Buy to maintain its own sales gains, Lasser said. He compared the situation to when <a class="inline_quotes" data-gdsid="46115" data-inline-quote-symbol="UAA" href="//www.cnbc.com/quotes/?symbol=UAA">Under Armour</a> started selling at <a class="inline_quotes" data-gdsid="23110" data-inline-quote-symbol="KSS" href="//www.cnbc.com/quotes/?symbol=KSS">Kohl's</a>, hurting sales of the athletic apparel brand at <a class="inline_quotes" data-gdsid="15235" data-inline-quote-symbol="DKS" href="//www.cnbc.com/quotes/?symbol=DKS">Dick's Sporting Goods</a>. "Apple's moves might not be as draconian for Best Buy," he said. "But, they do illustrate a risk."</p>, <p>A Best Buy spokesman didn't immediately return CNBC's request for comment.</p>, <p>Best Buy shares closed down more than 2 percent Thursday. The stock has tumbled 23 percent from a year ago.</p>, <p>Other <a href="/2019/01/03/us-companies-are-flashing-warning-signs-about-the-chinese-economy.html">businesses in the U.S. ranging from Ford to Tiffany have already started flashing warning signs</a> about the Chinese economy.</p>, <p>"Given that concerns around global demand have been a primary driver of the mass sell-off last year, it seems fair to assume that any further indications of slowdown in China should simply add fuel to the fire of fear," Nomura Instinet analyst Simeon Siegel told CNBC.</p>, <p></p>, <p><img src="//sc.cnbcfm.com/applications/cnbc.com/staticcontent/img/loader-2.gif"/></p>, <p class="label">Playing</p>, <p class="label_share">Share this video...</p>, <p class="label">Watch Next...</p>, <p class="Footer-sectionInfo">Got a confidential news tip? [<p>Supported by</p>, <p class="css-16vrk19 e1x1pwtg1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">By<!-- --> <a class="css-1riqqik e1x1pwtg0" href="https://www.nytimes.com/by/palko-karasz"><span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">Palko Karasz</span></a></p>, <p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">LONDON \u2014 For travel bargain hunters, the first day of 2019 was a spectacular one.</p>, <p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">On routes between Asia and North America, Cathay Pacific, the Hong Kong-based airline, offered seats in its exclusive business and first classes for less than the economy fare. A round-trip flight in business class from Vietnam to New York cost as little as $674.63, a small fraction of the usual price, according to One Mile at a Time, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://onemileatatime.com/cathay-pacific-cheap-business-class-fare/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">a travel blog</a> that documented the fares.</p>, <p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">\u201cCathay Pacific does actually do some quite aggressive fare sales from Asia,\u201d said Rob Burgess, the editor of a frequent flier website, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.headforpoints.com/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">headforpoints.com</a>. Business class seats from Asia to the United States can be found for around $5,000.</p>, <p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">But not this aggressive.</p>, <p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">The low prices, offered by mistake for several hours on Monday in North America, caught Cathay Pacific itself by surprise.</p>, <p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">On Wednesday, the airline acknowledged the error but said it would honor the fares. On Thursday, the trip that had briefly cost less than $700 appeared on the Cathay Pacific website for about $16,000 for a fully flexible fare.</p>, <p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">Mr. Burgess said that ticketing errors happen often when airlines deal with bookings to hundreds of destinations and in dozens of currencies. These processes work automatically but, \u201csomewhere in the system there\u2019s a human typing a number in,\u201d he said, which can lead to mistakes like mixing up currencies or leaving a zero off a price.</p>, <p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">Cathay Pacific did not say how many people had been quick enough to take advantage of the accidental bargain.</p>, <p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">\u201cYes \u2014 we made a mistake but we look forward to welcoming you on board with your ticket issued,\u201d the airline wrote on <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://twitter.com/cathaypacific/status/1080373505806426114" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">Twitter</a>, with the hashtags \u201c#promisemadepromisekept,\u201d and \u201c#lessonlearnt.\u201d</p>, <p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">Airlines tend to catch such glitches in a matter of hours, leaving the opportunity open to only the most up-to-date shoppers. Carriers that do not honor such tickets refund the purchase prices, so there is little downside to snapping them up.</p>, <p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">\u201cThese tickets are always booked by the same people,\u201d Mr. Burgess said \u2014 travel enthusiasts who, he said, often monitor travel websites obsessively. \u201dThere\u2019s no risk at all as long as you are smart enough not to book a hotel\u201d or other nonrefundable travel associated with the tickets.</p>, <p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">A competing carrier, Hong Kong Airlines, made a similar mistake last August, selling $600 business-class seats on flights from Asia to North America. The airline honored the fares, possibly setting an example for Cathay Pacific to follow, travel writers <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.forbes.com/sites/grantmartin/2019/01/02/cathay-pacific-accidentally-sells-international-business-class-fares-for-the-price-of-economy/#14ce41b1139d" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">commented</a>.</p>, <p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">For Cathay Pacific, this wasn\u2019t the first glitch in the past year. In October, the airline said a data breach had exposed the personal details and travel histories of <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/10/25/business/cathay-pacific-hack.html?module=inline" title="">as many as 9.4 million people</a>.</p>, <p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">While the airline has so far kept the cause and cost of the glitch quiet, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.scmp.com/news/hong-kong/hong-kong-economy/article/2180367/cathay-pacific-customer-who-bought-first-class" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">the mistake may result in a six-figure loss</a> or more, The South China Morning Post reported. Along with the tickets themselves, the rare bargain included the accompanying mileage credit, giving buyers discounted credit toward frequent flier status, with lounge access and other benefits.</p>, <p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">Cathay Pacific, known for its long-haul routes from its hub in Hong Kong, is <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://skytraxratings.com/airlines/cathay-pacific-airways-rating" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">regularly rated</a> among the world\u2019s best airlines. Its flatbed business and first-class seats with big-screen entertainment and fine dining have received praise from frequent travelers. But the carrier has been facing growing competition in Asia and recorded losses in 2016, 2017 and the first half of 2018.</p>, <p><i>Follow Palko Karasz on Twitter: </i><a href="https://twitter.com/karaszpalko"><i>@karaszpalko</i></a><i>.</i></p>, <p class="css-19n3nxx etcg8100"></p>, <p class="css-19n3nxx etcg8100"></p>, <p class="css-19n3nxx etcg8100"></p>, <p>Advertisement</p>]

Pacific Gas &amp; Electric Co (NYSE:PCG)\u2019s price to earnings ratio stood at 43.51. A high P/E suggests that investors are expecting higher earnings growth in the future compared to companies with a lower P/E. A low P/E can indicate either that a company may currently be undervalued or that the company is doing exceptionally well relative to its past trends.</p>, <p>The PEG ratio is used to determine a stock\u2019s value while taking the company\u2019s earnings growth into account, and is considered to provide a more complete picture than the P/E. [<p>\n\t\xa0Facebook\u2019s essential facility is unclear \u2013 is it a social network; a publisher of video, text, pictures and games; a communications service; or an advertising agency?</p>, <p>\n\tIn July last year, a week after she fined Google \u20ac4.34bn (\xa33.9bn) for\xa0 breaching antitrust rules, the EU competition commissioner, Margrethe Vestager, appeared on <em>Anger Management</em>, a podcast run by the former deputy prime minister Nick Clegg. It was not the first time they had spoken \u2013 the European Commission\u2019s list of meetings shows they met officially in January 2017 \u2013 but Clegg\u2019s public chat with the regulator most feared by Big Tech may have landed him a job. Less than three months later, Facebook announced that Clegg had been appointed as its new head of global affairs and communications.\n<p>\n\tThe following month, amid many fewer column inches, Facebook made two more significant hires: Johan Keetelaar, director of telecommunications at the Authority for Consumers and Markets in the Netherlands, and Kate Patchen, who led the US department of justice\u2019s antitrust section in San Francisco \u2013 in other words, two of the most senior competition regulators in Europe and the US. Facebook has hired top lawmakers in the past, including Timothy Muris, former chairman of the US Federal Trade Commission, and Kevin Martin, former chair of the Federal Communications Commission. But this activity has increased as the tech giant is accused of obliterating the press, spreading hatred and misinformation, and operating a monopoly.\n<p>\n\tIt is this last point that is most important to lawmakers. Claims that Facebook has swayed elections or even played a role in genocide are appalling, but difficult to prove. A monopoly is quantifiable. Facebook has a market share of 77 per cent of social media use in Europe, not including Instagram, which it owns. The original uses of the mobile phone, speech and messaging, have largely been captured by WhatsApp, which serves 65 billion messages and 3,800 years\u2019 worth of calls a day, and is owned, again, by Facebook.\xa0<div class="dianomi-ad dianomi-ad-2"><iframe height="150" scrolling="NO" src="//www.dianomi.com/smartads.epl?id=4302" style="width: 100%; border: none; overflow: hidden;" width="500"></iframe></div>\n<p>\n\tWhen Mark Zuckerberg appeared before Congress in April 2018, the Republican senator Lindsey Graham asked him to name his main competitor, or a product that consumers could choose instead. As Zuckerberg stalled, Graham asked if Facebook was a monopoly. The best Zuckerberg could offer was that it \u201cdoesn\u2019t feel like that to me\u201d.\n<p>\n\tThe documents seized in late November by Damian Collins, chair of the culture, media and sport select committee, detail a pattern of activity at Facebook that certainly feels anti-competitive. Competitors that used Facebook\u2019s Onavo app \u2013 sold as a security measure \u2013 were monitored. Commercial partners were \u201cwhitelisted\u201d for special access to user data. On 24 January 2013, Twitter \u2013 Facebook \u2018s biggest competitor for social media market share \u2013 launched an app called Vine, which enabled users to post short, shareable videos. The same day, Facebook\u2019s vice president Justin Osofsky emailed Mark Zuckerberg to warn him that Vine users could \u201cfind friends via FB [Facebook]\u201d and to suggest they \u201cshut down their friends API access today\u201d \u2013 throttling the data that Vine could receive from its own users. Zuckerberg replied: \u201cYup, go for it.\u201d\n<p>\n\tFacebook is also a new kind of company \u2013 global, nebulous and constantly changing. The economist Jean Tirole points out that natural monopolies occur when a technology\u2019s \u201cessential facility\u201d cannot realistically be duplicated. Railway lines, an energy grid or a telephone network all do one job for which it is very hard to create a competing alternative. Last century, it was possible to break up Standard Oil and AT&amp;T into smaller entities without affecting the services they provided. Yet Facebook\u2019s essential facility is less clear \u2013 is it a social network; a publisher of video, text, pictures and games; a communications service; or an advertising agency? Nor can it be separated into equally valuable components; Facebook, Messenger, Instagram, WhatsApp, Oculus and others are interdependent, and the data they share is crucial to Facebook\u2019s dominance.<div class="dianomi-ad dianomi-ad-1"><iframe height="150" scrolling="NO" src="//www.dianomi.com/smartads.epl?id=3585" style="width: 100%; border: none; overflow: hidden;" width="500"></iframe></div>\n<p>\n\tThere is also the question of who gets to regulate Facebook, and how. The EU can issue heavy fines, but a company that serves 2.2 billion people around the world is a slippery thing to regulate. This is why Facebook, despite having made \xa31.3bn from advertising sales in the UK last year, paid just \xa37.4m net tax (0.62 per cent).\n<p>\n\tThe political will to tame Facebook may also waver. Donald Trump has said that his administration is \u201ccertainly looking\u201d at antitrust allegations, but his new North American Free Trade Agreement gives Facebook more power to challenge US regulators. It must also be asked why he would regulate a company credited with assisting his rise to power.\n<p>\n\tYet this does not mean that 2019 will be a great year for Facebook, because there are still two agents who can do more than any regulator to curtail Facebook\u2019s power. These are the agents who gave it that power in the first place: the user and the investor.<div class="dianomi-ad dianomi-ad-3"><iframe height="150" scrolling="NO" src="//www.dianomi.com/smartads.epl?id=4303" style="width: 100%; border: none; overflow: hidden;" width="500"></iframe></div>\n<p>\n\tOn 25 July last year, Facebook\u2019s total stock market capitalisation was over $620bn (\xa3490bn). This is as much as $500bn (\xa3395bn) more than all the revenue it has earned since it became cash-flow positive in 2009. On 26 July, Facebook published quarterly results that showed slower-than-expected growth of both revenue and users. The company\u2019s stock fell 20 per cent, wiping $120bn (\xa390.8bn) off its market cap; it has since fallen to its lowest level for more than 18 months. In less than an hour, Zuckerberg lost an amount of money comparable to the yearly GDP of Cambodia.\n<p>\n\tIn September, Pew Research published a survey of 3,400 Facebook users which found that 44 per cent of the 18-29 age group had deleted the Facebook app from their phone in the past year. This, more than anything else, should be Facebook\u2019s wake-up call; nearly every trend in internet use has been led by the young. These users are the product Facebook sells to its clients, and they appear to be leaving in droves.\n<p>\n\tThis could be because of Facebook\u2019s many scandals. But\xa0 monopolies are also dull. And on the internet, sold to the young as a cure for boredom, no regulator is as powerful as the simple decision to switch off.<div class="video-inread unruly-video">\n<div id="1x3">\n<script type="text/javascript">\r\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\tgoogletag.cmd.push(function() { googletag.display("1x3"); Last century, it was possible to break up Standard Oil and AT&amp;T into smaller entities without affecting the services they provided. Yet Facebook\u2019s essential facility is less clear \u2013 is it a social network; a publisher of video, text, pictures and games; a communications service; or an advertising agency? Nor can it be separated into equally valuable components; Facebook, Messenger, Instagram, WhatsApp, Oculus and others are interdependent, and the data they share is crucial to Facebook\u2019s dominance.<div class="dianomi-ad dianomi-ad-1"><iframe height="150" scrolling="NO" src="//www.dianomi.com/smartads.epl?id=3585" style="width: 100%; border: none; overflow: hidden;" width="500"></iframe></div>\n<p>\n\tThere is also the question of who gets to regulate Facebook, and how. The EU can issue heavy fines, but a company that serves 2.2 billion people around the world is a slippery thing to regulate. This is why Facebook, despite having made \xa31.3bn from advertising sales in the UK last year, paid just \xa37.4m net tax (0.62 per cent).\n<p>\n\tThe political will to tame Facebook may also waver. Donald Trump has said that his administration is \u201ccertainly looking\u201d at antitrust allegations, but his new North American Free Trade Agreement gives Facebook more power to challenge US regulators. It must also be asked why he would regulate a company credited with assisting his rise to power.\n<p>\n\tYet this does not mean that 2019 will be a great year for Facebook, because there are still two agents who can do more than any regulator to curtail Facebook\u2019s power. These are the agents who gave it that power in the first place: the user and the investor.<div class="dianomi-ad dianomi-ad-3"><iframe height="150" scrolling="NO" src="//www.dianomi.com/smartads.epl?id=4303" style="width: 100%; border: none; overflow: hidden;" width="500"></iframe></div>\n<p>\n\tOn 25 July last year, Facebook\u2019s total stock market capitalisation was over $620bn (\xa3490bn). This is as much as $500bn (\xa3395bn) more than all the revenue it has earned since it became cash-flow positive in 2009. On 26 July, Facebook published quarterly results that showed slower-than-expected growth of both revenue and users. The company\u2019s stock fell 20 per cent, wiping $120bn (\xa390.8bn) off its market cap; it has since fallen to its lowest level for more than 18 months. In less than an hour, Zuckerberg lost an amount of money comparable to the yearly GDP of Cambodia.\n<p>\n\tIn September, Pew Research published a survey of 3,400 Facebook users which found that 44 per cent of the 18-29 age group had deleted the Facebook app from their phone in the past year. This, more than anything else, should be Facebook\u2019s wake-up call; nearly every trend in internet use has been led by the young. These users are the product Facebook sells to its clients, and they appear to be leaving in droves.\n<p>\n\tThis could be because of Facebook\u2019s many scandals. But\xa0 monopolies are also dull. And on the internet, sold to the young as a cure for boredom, no regulator is as powerful as the simple decision to switch off.<div class="video-inread unruly-video">\n<div id="1x3">\n<script type="text/javascript">\r\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\tgoogletag.cmd.push(function() { googletag.display("1x3"); });\r\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t  </script>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>\n<strong>Read all the pieces in our \u201c2019 \u2013 the big questions\u201d\xa0series:\xa0</strong>\n<p>\n<a href="https://www.newstatesman.com/world/north-america/2019/01/2019-big-questions-will-trump-go-war"><strong>John Bew: Will Trump go to war?</strong></a>\n<p>\n<a href="https://www.newstatesman.com/politics/economy/2019/01/2019-big-questions-will-there-be-another-crash"><strong>Felix Martin: Will there be another crash?</strong></a><div class="dianomi-ad dianomi-ad-3"><iframe height="150" scrolling="NO" src="//www.dianomi.com/smartads.epl?id=4303" style="width: 100%; border: none; overflow: hidden;" width="500"></iframe></div>\n<p>\n<a href="https://www.newstatesman.com/politics/uk/2019/01/2019-big-questions-will-brexit-actually-happen"><strong>George Eaton: Will Brexit actually happen?</strong></a>\n<p>\n<a href="https://www.newstatesman.com/culture/tv-radio/2019/01/2019-big-questions-are-tv-channels-irrelevant"><strong>Anna Leszkiewicz: Are TV channels irrelevant?</strong></a>\n<p>\n<a href="https://www.newstatesman.com/world/europe/2019/01/2019-big-questions-will-far-right-triumph-europe"><b>Paul Mason: Will the far right triumph in Europe?</b></a>\n</p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p>, <p>\n\tIt is this last point that is most important to lawmakers. Claims that Facebook has swayed elections or even played a role in genocide are appalling, but difficult to prove. A monopoly is quantifiable. Facebook has a market share of 77 per cent of social media use in Europe, not including Instagram, which it owns. The original uses of the mobile phone, speech and messaging, have largely been captured by WhatsApp, which serves 65 billion messages and 3,800 years\u2019 worth of calls a day, and is owned, again, by Facebook.\xa0<div class="dianomi-ad dianomi-ad-2"><iframe height="150" scrolling="NO" src="//www.dianomi.com/smartads.epl?id=4302" style="width: 100%; border: none; overflow: hidden;" width="500"></iframe></div>\n<p>\n\tWhen Mark Zuckerberg appeared before Congress in April 2018, the Republican senator Lindsey Graham asked him to name his main competitor, or a product that consumers could choose instead. As Zuckerberg stalled, Graham asked if Facebook was a monopoly. The best Zuckerberg could offer was that it \u201cdoesn\u2019t feel like that to me\u201d.\n<p>\n\tThe documents seized in late November by Damian Collins, chair of the culture, media and sport select committee, detail a pattern of activity at Facebook that certainly feels anti-competitive. Competitors that used Facebook\u2019s Onavo app \u2013 sold as a security measure \u2013 were monitored. Commercial partners were \u201cwhitelisted\u201d for special access to user data. On 24 January 2013, Twitter \u2013 Facebook \u2018s biggest competitor for social media market share \u2013 launched an app called Vine, which enabled users to post short, shareable videos. The same day, Facebook\u2019s vice president Justin Osofsky emailed Mark Zuckerberg to warn him that Vine users could \u201cfind friends via FB [Facebook]\u201d and to suggest they \u201cshut down their friends API access today\u201d \u2013 throttling the data that Vine could receive from its own users. Zuckerberg replied: \u201cYup, go for it.\u201d\n<p>\n\tFacebook is also a new kind of company \u2013 global, nebulous and constantly changing. The economist Jean Tirole points out that natural monopolies occur when a technology\u2019s \u201cessential facility\u201d cannot realistically be duplicated. Railway lines, an energy grid or a telephone network all do one job for which it is very hard to create a competing alternative. Last century, it was possible to break up Standard Oil and AT&amp;T into smaller entities without affecting the services they provided. Yet Facebook\u2019s essential facility is less clear \u2013 is it a social network; a publisher of video, text, pictures and games; a communications service; or an advertising agency? Nor can it be separated into equally valuable components; Facebook, Messenger, Instagram, WhatsApp, Oculus and others are interdependent, and the data they share is crucial to Facebook\u2019s dominance.<div class="dianomi-ad dianomi-ad-1"><iframe height="150" scrolling="NO" src="//www.dianomi.com/smartads.epl?id=3585" style="width: 100%; border: none; overflow: hidden;" width="500"></iframe></div>\n<p>\n\tThere is also the question of who gets to regulate Facebook, and how. The EU can issue heavy fines, but a company that serves 2.2 billion people around the world is a slippery thing to regulate. This is why Facebook, despite having made \xa31.3bn from advertising sales in the UK last year, paid just \xa37.4m net tax (0.62 per cent).\n<p>\n\tThe political will to tame Facebook may also waver. It must also be asked why he would regulate a company credited with assisting his rise to power.\n<p>\n\tYet this does not mean that 2019 will be a great year for Facebook, because there are still two agents who can do more than any regulator to curtail Facebook\u2019s power. These are the agents who gave it that power in the first place: the user and the investor.<div class="dianomi-ad dianomi-ad-3"><iframe height="150" scrolling="NO" src="//www.dianomi.com/smartads.epl?id=4303" style="width: 100%; border: none; overflow: hidden;" width="500"></iframe></div>\n<p>\n\tOn 25 July last year, Facebook\u2019s total stock market capitalisation was over $620bn (\xa3490bn). And on the internet, sold to the young as a cure for boredom, no regulator is as powerful as the simple decision to switch off.<div class="video-inread unruly-video">\n<div id="1x3">\n<script type="text/javascript">\r\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\tgoogletag.cmd.push(function() { googletag.display("1x3"); });\r\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t  </script>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>\n<strong>Read all the pieces in our \u201c2019 \u2013 the big questions\u201d\xa0series:\xa0</strong>\n<p>\n<a href="https://www.newstatesman.com/world/north-america/2019/01/2019-big-questions-will-trump-go-war"><strong>John Bew: Will Trump go to war?</strong></a>\n<p>\n<a href="https://www.newstatesman.com/politics/economy/2019/01/2019-big-questions-will-there-be-another-crash"><strong>Felix Martin: Will there be another crash?</strong></a><div class="dianomi-ad dianomi-ad-3"><iframe height="150" scrolling="NO" src="//www.dianomi.com/smartads.epl?id=4303" style="width: 100%; border: none; overflow: hidden;" width="500"></iframe></div>\n<p>\n<a href="https://www.newstatesman.com/politics/uk/2019/01/2019-big-questions-will-brexit-actually-happen"><strong>George Eaton: Will Brexit actually happen?</strong></a>\n<p>\n<a href="https://www.newstatesman.com/culture/tv-radio/2019/01/2019-big-questions-are-tv-channels-irrelevant"><strong>Anna Leszkiewicz: Are TV channels irrelevant?</strong></a>\n<p>\n<a href="https://www.newstatesman.com/world/europe/2019/01/2019-big-questions-will-far-right-triumph-europe"><b>Paul Mason: Will the far right triumph in Europe?</b></a>\n</p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p>, <p>\n\tThere is also the question of who gets to regulate Facebook, and how. The EU can issue heavy fines, but a company that serves 2.2 billion people around the world is a slippery thing to regulate. This is why Facebook, despite having made \xa31.3bn from advertising sales in the UK last year, paid just \xa37.4m net tax (0.62 per cent).\n<p>\n\tThe political will to tame Facebook may also waver. Donald Trump has said that his administration is \u201ccertainly looking\u201d at antitrust allegations, but his new North American Free Trade Agreement gives Facebook more power to challenge US regulators. It must also be asked why he would regulate a company credited with assisting his rise to power.\n<p>\n\tYet this does not mean that 2019 will be a great year for Facebook, because there are still two agents who can do more than any regulator to curtail Facebook\u2019s power. These are the agents who gave it that power in the first place: the user and the investor.<div class="dianomi-ad dianomi-ad-3"><iframe height="150" scrolling="NO" src="//www.dianomi.com/smartads.epl?id=4303" style="width: 100%; border: none; overflow: hidden;" width="500"></iframe></div>\n<p>\n\tOn 25 July last year, Facebook\u2019s total stock market capitalisation was over $620bn (\xa3490bn). This is as much as $500bn (\xa3395bn) more than all the revenue it has earned since it became cash-flow positive in 2009. On 26 July, Facebook published quarterly results that showed slower-than-expected growth of both revenue and users. The company\u2019s stock fell 20 per cent, wiping $120bn (\xa390.8bn) off its market cap; it has since fallen to its lowest level for more than 18 months. In less than an hour, Zuckerberg lost an amount of money comparable to the yearly GDP of Cambodia.\n<p>\n\tIn September, Pew Research published a survey of 3,400 Facebook users which found that 44 per cent of the 18-29 age group had deleted the Facebook app from their phone in the past year. This, more than anything else, should be Facebook\u2019s wake-up call; nearly every trend in internet use has been led by the young. These users are the product Facebook sells to its clients, and they appear to be leaving in droves.\n<p>\n\tThis could be because of Facebook\u2019s many scandals. But\xa0 monopolies are also dull. And on the internet, sold to the young as a cure for boredom, no regulator is as powerful as the simple decision to switch off.<div class="video-inread unruly-video">\n<div id="1x3">\n<script type="text/javascript">\r\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\tgoogletag.cmd.push(function() { googletag.display("1x3"); });\r\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t  </script>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>\n<strong>Read all the pieces in our \u201c2019 \u2013 the big questions\u201d\xa0series:\xa0</strong>\n<p>\n<a href="https://www.newstatesman.com/world/north-america/2019/01/2019-big-questions-will-trump-go-war"><strong>John Bew: Will Trump go to war?</strong></a>\n<p>\n<a href="https://www.newstatesman.com/politics/economy/2019/01/2019-big-questions-will-there-be-another-crash"><strong>Felix Martin: Will there be another crash?</strong></a><div class="dianomi-ad dianomi-ad-3"><iframe height="150" scrolling="NO" src="//www.dianomi.com/smartads.epl?id=4303" style="width: 100%; border: none; overflow: hidden;" width="500"></iframe></div>\n<p>\n<a href="https://www.newstatesman.com/politics/uk/2019/01/2019-big-questions-will-brexit-actually-happen"><strong>George Eaton: Will Brexit actually happen?</strong></a>\n<p>\n<a href="https://www.newstatesman.com/culture/tv-radio/2019/01/2019-big-questions-are-tv-channels-irrelevant"><strong>Anna Leszkiewicz: Are TV channels irrelevant?</strong></a>\n<p>\n<a href="https://www.newstatesman.com/world/europe/2019/01/2019-big-questions-will-far-right-triumph-europe"><b>Paul Mason: Will the far right triumph in Europe?</b></a>\n</p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p></p>, <p>\n\tThe political will to tame Facebook may also waver. [<p class="BylineBar_reading-time" style="color:undefined">4 Min Read</p>, <p>NEW YORK (Reuters) - Wall Street plunged on Thursday after slowing U.S. factory activity on the heels of a dire revenue warning from Apple Inc (<span id="\u201dsymbol_AAPL.O_0\u201d"><a href="/finance/stocks/overview?symbol=AAPL.O">AAPL.O</a></span>) fueled fears of a global economic slowdown. </p>, <p>The magnitude of Apple\u2019s holiday quarter revenue shortfall sent shockwaves through the technology sector, which pulled all three major U.S. stock indexes down more than 2 percent, with the Nasdaq posting a 3 percent loss. </p>, <p>S&amp;P Technology companies .SPLRCT slid 5.1 percent, its biggest one-day percentage drop since August 2011. The Philadelphia SE Semiconductor index .SOX ended the session 5.9 percent lower. </p>, <p>Late Wednesday, Apple chief executive Tim Cook wrote in a letter to investors that the company had not foreseen the extent of China\u2019s economic deceleration, which was exacerbated by U.S.-China trade tensions. The iPhone maker\u2019s shares dropped 10.0 percent. </p>, <p>A report from the Institute for Supply Management showed U.S. factory activity USPMI=ECI in December suffered the biggest drop since October 2008, the height of the financial crisis. Its PMI reading, while still in expansion territory, hit its lowest level in more than two years.  </p>, <p>\u201cThe Chinese slowdown was expected but today\u2019s softer-than-expected ISM number took investors by surprise because the U.S. seemed to be the only port in the storm,\u201d said Sam Stovall, chief investment strategist of CFRA Research in New York. \u201cBut now it appears that our economic growth is facing trade related headwinds.\u201d  </p>, <p>\u201cInvestors are worried that this is an indication that things could be getting worse from here and Apple is only the tip of the iceberg,\u201d Stovall added. </p>, <p>Major automakers reported weak U.S. new car sales in December, with Ford Motor Co (<span id="\u201dsymbol_F.N_3\u201d"><a href="/finance/stocks/overview?symbol=F.N">F.N</a></span>) and General Motors Co (<span id="\u201dsymbol_GM.N_4\u201d"><a href="/finance/stocks/overview?symbol=GM.N">GM.N</a></span>) reporting sales falling by 8.8 percent and 2.7 percent, respectively. Ford shares fell 1.5 percent, while GM dropped 4.1 percent. </p>, <p>The Dow Jones Industrial Average <a href="/finance/markets/index?symbol=.DJI">.DJI</a> fell 660.02 points, or 2.83 percent, to 22,686.22, the S&amp;P 500 <a href="/finance/markets/index?symbol=.SPX">.SPX</a> lost 62.14 points, or 2.48 percent, to 2,447.89 and the Nasdaq Composite <a href="/finance/markets/index?symbol=.IXIC">.IXIC</a> dropped 202.43 points, or 3.04 percent, to 6,463.50. </p>, <p>Of the 11 major sectors in the S&amp;P 500, all but defensive real estate .SPLRCR and utilities .SPLRCU stocks closed in the red. </p>, <p>Trade-sensitive industrials also weighed on the Dow, led by Caterpillar Inc (<span id="\u201dsymbol_CAT.N_10\u201d"><a href="/finance/stocks/overview?symbol=CAT.N">CAT.N</a></span>), 3M Co (<span id="\u201dsymbol_MMM.N_11\u201d"><a href="/finance/stocks/overview?symbol=MMM.N">MMM.N</a></span>) and Boeing Co (<span id="\u201dsymbol_BA.N_12\u201d"><a href="/finance/stocks/overview?symbol=BA.N">BA.N</a></span>). </p>, <p>Bristol-Myers Squibb Co (<span id="\u201dsymbol_BMY.N_13\u201d"><a href="/finance/stocks/overview?symbol=BMY.N">BMY.N</a></span>) shares dropped 13.3 percent after the drugmaker announced plans to buy rival Celgene Corp (<span id="\u201dsymbol_CELG.O_14\u201d"><a href="/finance/stocks/overview?symbol=CELG.O">CELG.O</a></span>) for about $74 billion. Celgene shares jumped 20.7 percent on the news.  </p>, <p>Shares of U.S. commercial air carriers slid after Delta Air Lines (<span id="\u201dsymbol_DAL.N_15\u201d"><a href="/finance/stocks/overview?symbol=DAL.N">DAL.N</a></span>) cut its fourth quarter revenue estimate. The S&amp;P 1500 Airlines index .SPCOMAIR sank 5.9 percent. </p>, <p>Yields on 2-year Treasuries dipped below the federal funds effective rate for the first time since 2008, a move many believe suggests the central bank will not be able to continue its monetary tightening policy. The outlook for higher rates has been considered a headwind to equities in recent months. </p>, <p>Declining issues outnumbered advancing ones on the NYSE by a 1.39-to-1 ratio; on Nasdaq, a 2.28-to-1 ratio favored decliners. </p>, <p>The S&amp;P 500 posted no new 52-week highs and 13 new lows; the Nasdaq Composite recorded 6 new highs and 48 new lows.   </p>, <p>Volume on U.S. exchanges was 8.11 billion shares, compared to the 9.16 billion average over the last 20 trading days.    </p>, <p class="Attribution_content">Reporting by Stephen Culp; Editing by Phil Berlowitz</p>, <p><span>All quotes delayed a minimum of 15 minutes. [<p>The mall used to be one of the few places teens were free to hang out far from prying parental eyes. That\u2019s no longer the case at Water Tower Place on the Magnificent Mile, the latest mall to ban unsupervised teens on Friday and Saturday evenings in an attempt to crack down on disruptive incidents.</p>, <p>From 4 p.m. until the mall closes at 9 p.m. on Fridays and Saturdays, trained public safety officers will card visitors at the Magnificent Mile shopping center\u2019s entrances, mall owner Brookfield Properties said Thursday.</p>, <p>Anyone without a valid photo ID confirming they are at least 18 years old won\u2019t be allowed into the mall unless they are accompanied by a parent or other adult who is at least 21 years old,  Brookfield said in a news release.</p>, <p>The policy \u201cis intended to help provide a safe, peaceful experience at our shopping center,\u201d Mitch Feldman, senior general manager of Water Tower Place, said in the news release.</p>, <p>The announcement comes less than a week after a Saturday evening incident involving about 60 young people that gathered on North Michigan Avenue and forced the mall to close an hour early, said Ald. Brian Hopkins, 2nd.</p>, <p>A handful of the teens were believed to have been involved in an attack on people in the nearby Chicago Avenue Red Line station shortly before 7:15 p.m. Saturday,  Hopkins said. Victims suffered bone fractures, cuts and bruises, police said in a news release.</p>, <p>Felony charges have been filed against one of several teens who went on a rampage at a Near North Side <a href="/topic/transportation/public-transportation/chicago-transit-authority-ORGOV000082-topic.html">CTA</a> stop last weekend, kicking and punching people, according to Chicago police.</p>, <p>The 15-year-old boy is charged with aggravated battery and mob action, the department said in a release.</p>, <p>Police...</p>, <p>\u201cThis stood out as an aberration because the injuries were severe,\u201d Hopkins said. \u201cBut this type of incident, involving large numbers of juveniles engaged in hostile attacks that appear to be random, happens with disturbing frequency.\u201d</p>, <p>Most of the incidents he hears about are more minor and aren\u2019t always reported,  he said.</p>, <p>Brookfield said in an email that disruptive behavior from unsupervised teens  has been an issue at Water Tower Place in recent months and it decided to move ahead with a formal curfew after the  Saturday incident. The program  also will help eliminate distractions for security officers at the mall, Brookfield said.</p>, <p>Water Tower Place is far from the first mall to require adult supervision. The Mall of America in Bloomington, Minn., which, like Water Tower Place, is a major tourist destination, has had a similar policy since 1996.</p>, <p>At the Mall of America, children under age 16 must be accompanied by an adult who is at least 21 after 4 p.m. on Fridays and Saturdays, and the policy was enforced all day between Dec. 26 and 31.</p>, <p>Brookfield has similar policies at a handful of other properties, but Water Tower Place is its first in Illinois to adopt one.</p>, <p>Starting Friday afternoon, public safety officers in the mall will check the identification of anyone who appears to be under 18 years old. Valid IDs must be tamper-proof and include a photo and date of birth. Acceptable forms of identification may include state-issued driver\u2019s licenses or ID cards, military IDs, school IDs or passports. Shoppers will be offered a wristband if they\u2019ve passed an ID check.</p>, <p>Each adult can accompany up to four minors between ages 10 and 17. There is no limit on the number of kids under 10 one adult can supervise.</p>, <p>For about six years, west suburban North Riverside Park Mall has restricted unsupervised teens\u2019 access to the mall after 6 p.m. on Fridays and Saturdays. Last weekend, the mall was forced to close several hours early on Saturday when large groups of young people caused disturbances that resulted in two arrests, said Harvey Ahitow, the mall\u2019s general manager.</p>, <p>On Wednesday, the mall again stepped up security measures and began barring unsupervised minors at 11 a.m. in response to social media threats suggesting more incidents. There were no incidents, he said.</p>, <p>Incidents are more common on holiday weekends, and social media has made it easier for large groups to organize, he said.</p>, <p>\u201cIn my mind, we would absolutely have more trouble if we weren\u2019t controlling the kids coming into the center,\u201d  Ahitow said.</p>, <p>The mall initially received some pushback from its movie theater and granted it an exemption. Some food court tenants also worried about losing business. But overall, Ahitow said he\u2019s received few complaints.</p>, <p>\u201cWhen you create a more peaceful environment, it stands to reason you have more people going in who are going to shop,\u201d he said.</p>, <p>Water Tower Place has a number of tenants targeting young customers, including Forever 21, Hollister Co. and American Eagle Outfitters. Arcade and bar Dave &amp; Buster\u2019s is  seeking to move its Gold Coast location to the mall.</p>, <p>Asked if he worried the ID checks would be a turnoff for tourists or teen shoppers, Hopkins said he wants Water Tower to succeed but approaches the issue \u201csolely from a public safety standpoint.\u201d</p>, <p>John Chikow, president and CEO of the Magnificent Mile Association, said it\u2019s too soon to say how the businesses  the group represents will react, or whether the policy will prevent disturbances in the shopping district.</p>, <p>\u201cWe stand behind our members determining what\u2019s best for their business in terms of creating a positive experience for their patrons,\u201d Chikow said.</p>, <p>The American Civil Liberties Union of Illinois has concerns about how malls like Water Tower Place apply age restrictions,  spokesman Ed Yohnka said.</p>, <p>\u201cIs it going to be applied equally to young people from the North Side of Chicago attending a movie as to a group from the South and West sides coming because one wants to buy a gift for their girlfriend or mother? Once these kinds of rules are in place, there\u2019s too much of an opportunity here for some sort of racial inequality,\u201d he said.</p>, <p><em><a href="mailto:lzumbach@chicagotribune.com" target="_blank">lzumbach@chicagotribune.com</a></em></p>, <p><em>Twitter <a href="https://twitter.com/laurenzumbach" target="_blank">@laurenzumbach</a></em></p>, <p><strong>MORE COVERAGE</strong></p>, <p><strong><a href="https://www.chicagotribune.com/business/ct-ford-city-youth-escort-0107-biz-20150107-story.html">Ford City Mall to require supervision of teens on Friday, Saturday nights \xbb</a></strong></p>, <p><strong><a href="https://www.chicagotribune.com/business/ct-biz-chicago-loop-security-20180228-story.html">Armed private guards will patrol State Street to aid security downtown \xbb</a></strong></p>, <p><strong><a href="https://www.chicagotribune.com/business/ct-biz-wicker-park-bucktown-safety-patrol-20180318-story.html">Wicker Park, Bucktown businesses to use employees to patrol streets \xbb</a></strong></p>]

[<p class="byline__name byline__name--block">\n    \n      Esme Nicholson\n    \n  </p>, <p>\n                Berlin is a tech hub, but 70 percent of the city's businesses have complained to the city's Chamber of Commerce and Industry about inadequate broadband.\n                \n                <b aria-label="Image credit" class="credit">\n                    \n                    Rafael Dols/Getty Images\n                    \n                </b>\n<b class="hide-caption"><b>hide caption</b></b>\n</p>, <p class="caption">Berlin is a tech hub, but 70 percent of the city's businesses have complained to the city's Chamber of Commerce and Industry about inadequate broadband.</p>, <p>Berlin's flourishing tech scene attracts talent from across the globe. At a startup incubator in the western part of the city, an international team recently launched an app called SPRT, which connects sports enthusiasts.</p>, <p>Amy Cooper, a 20-year-old SPRT employee who moved to Germany from Britain last June, complains that Berlin's Internet speed is so slow, it feels like the old dial-up days she has heard her parents reminisce about.</p>, <p>"We're working in a coworking office where there's loads of startups, everything's online," Cooper says. "We use it every second of the day, so it's important for us that it works and that it's reliable. And most of the time, it's not, and that can be really frustrating."</p>, <p>Germany is Europe's largest economy, but business leaders warn it is in danger of losing its edge because of sluggish Internet connections. While other countries are thinking about whether to upgrade their cellphone systems to 5G, Germany is still grappling with 3G.</p>, <p>A <a href="https://www.dw.com/en/germany-land-of-woefully-slow-internet/a-42195241">report by Germany's Federal Network Agency</a> last year showed that 29 percent of German Internet users reported Internet speeds of less than half of what was promised by service providers.</p>, <p>Claudia Engfeld of the Berlin Chamber of Commerce and Industry says 70 percent of the city's businesses have complained to her office about slow and inadequate broadband. Engfeld says it's not only an issue for new tech companies, but also for the city's established <em>Mittelstand</em> (midsize) engineering firms.</p>, <p>"If you are doing 3D printing on an industrial level, you'll need to be fast," Engfeld says. "You can't wait two days till your machine has communicated to the printer to do something. This is a disadvantage that Berlin-based entrepreneurs have in comparison to other cities throughout the world."</p>, <p>The Chamber of Commerce <a href="https://breitbandatlas.berlin/">launched an online map </a>tracking where subpar broadband is affecting businesses in the city. Since last summer, more than 4,000 companies have contributed to the map, pinpointing areas with connectivity problems.</p>, <p>Engfeld says in some parts of Berlin, connections are so patchy that companies have had to move or ask staff to work from home, where Internet reliability is greater. Engfeld says having access to a good connection depends on luck, and how far you are from a distribution box.</p>, <p>\n                A cabinet containing components of a broadband network using optical fiber is on display at Germany's Ministry of Transport and Digital Infrastructure.\n                \n                <b aria-label="Image credit" class="credit">\n                    \n                    John MacDougall/AFP/Getty Images\n                    \n                </b>\n<b class="hide-caption"><b>hide caption</b></b>\n</p>, <p class="caption">A cabinet containing components of a broadband network using optical fiber is on display at Germany's Ministry of Transport and Digital Infrastructure.</p>, <p>Many CEOs blame Deutsche Telekom, the former state provider, which still dominates the domestic telecommunications market. Instead of installing fiber-optic cables, Deutsche Telekom has been fixing and improving its old copper telephone system, a process the telecommunications giant calls "vectoring."</p>, <p>Engfeld says relying on old technology this way is the problem.</p>, <p>"In Germany, you will find almost everywhere copper cable that's not capable to go faster than 250 megabits per second," she says. "The average reality is about 50 megabits per second. That's quite poor."</p>, <p>According to the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development, less than 2 percent of Germany's broadband connections are carried by pure fiber-optic systems. Deutsche Telekom spokesperson Georg von Wagner insists that most customers don't need anything near speeds of 1 gigabit per second, the standard speed Germany can offer with pure fiber optics.</p>, <p>"Of course we could have decided, four or five years ago, to get rid of the copper wires and we could have replaced them with fiber-optic cables. If we had gone this route, 10 to 15 percent of German households would now have access to download speeds of up to 1 gigabit a second," von Wagner says.</p>, <p>But, he says, fiber-optic coverage is expensive and would benefit relatively few customers. "The rest of the country would only have an average download connection of 16 megabits," he says. "Instead, by improving the old copper wiring, 80 percent of households in Germany have access to download speeds of up to 50 megabits per second."</p>, <p>Tech policy journalist Tomas Rudl says Deutsche Telekom's fiber-optic strategy \u2014 or lack of one \u2014 was shortsighted but was meant to save money.</p>, <p>"Deutsche Telekom's reasons for investing in the old copper wires makes complete financial sense," Rudl says. "It's much less expensive to rig the older tech than it is to dig deep into the ground and install fiber-optic cables."</p>, <p>He stresses that the government also has to take its share of the blame. "Although Deutsche Telekom was privatized, the state is still one of its major shareholders," Rudl says. "So it's within the government's own interests to ensure that its infrastructure policies and funding benefit a major German job provider."</p>, <p>But the government now sees the benefits and necessity of fiber optics. Chancellor Angela Merkel's cabinet has promised to help subsidize the rollout of fiber-optic broadband by 2025.</p>, <p>For Cooper at the SPRT startup, a 2025 rollout seems too little, too late. It's bad enough that the Internet goes down regularly at her office, but the creaky network also supports mobile phones and she is concerned SPRT's customers can't get the coverage they need to use the app she helped create.</p>, <p>"Should you really have to think about those things in Berlin," she asks, "one of the biggest and most important tech cities in Europe?"</p>, <p class="left">NPR thanks our sponsors</p>, <p class="right"><a href="/about-npr/186948703/corporate-sponsorship">Become an NPR sponsor</a></p>]

[<p class="Text_Body">Delta Air Lines' shares plunged the most in more than three years, pulling rivals lower, after disappointing ticket pricing forced the carrier to cut its revenue forecasts for the second time in two months.</p>, <p class="Text_Body">Fourth-quarter revenue from each seat flown a mile, or unit revenue, will rise 3 percent from a year earlier, the Atlanta-based airline said in a regulatory filing Thursday. Delta previously forecast a 3.5 percent gain.</p>, <p class="Text_Body">The news added to investor concerns that weaker pricing and the possibility for slowing global demand from trade wars could weigh on profits this year. The timing \u2014 coming only three weeks after a Delta update on Dec. 13 and following the busy holiday travel season \u2014 may have magnified investors' alarm, said Savanthi Syth, an analyst at Raymond James Financial Inc.</p>, <p class="Text_Body">"We do not believe that the sharp sell-off in the U.S. airline shares beyond general market weakness is warranted, albeit understandable given the optics on a day with ample supply of negative headlines," she said in a note. Delta's reduced revenue outlook doesn't indicate general industry weakness, Syth wrote.</p>, <p class="Text_Body">Buckingham Research Group analyst Dan McKenzie called Delta's adjustment "a blip, not a major revenue stumbling block."</p>, <p class="Text_Body">The unit-revenue change would reduce fourth-quarter profit by 4 cents from the $1.30 a share previously expected, according to Syth's estimates. [<p class="">January 1, 2019</p>, <p class="print-copy"></p>, <p>https://www.wsj.com/articles/isms-plunge-is-no-cause-for-panic-11546540162</p>, <p>How much attention should investors pay to manufacturers\u2019 sentimental journey?</p>, <p>Manufacturers certainly appear glum. The Institute for Supply Management reported that its index of factory activity<a class="icon none" href="https://www.wsj.com/articles/u-s-factory-sector-growth-pulled-back-in-december-11546530431?mod=article_inline"> fell to 54.1 in December</a> from November\u2019s 59.3\u2014well below the 57.9 economists had expected and the biggest drop in a decade.</p>, <p>This was bad news for a stock market already smarting after\n\n\n\n\n\n\n            <a href="https://quotes.wsj.com/AAPL">Apple</a>\n<span class="company-name-type"> Inc.</span>\n<a class="icon none" href="https://www.wsj.com/articles/apple-revises-guidance-sees-lower-revenue-in-fiscal-1st-quarter-11546465050?mod=article_inline"> slashed its quarterly revenue forecast </a>on shrinking Chinese demand, but less of a surprise as it seemed at first blush. Regional manufacturing surveys suggested the national ISM index was in for a stark drop, yet many may not have been incorporated in published forecasts for the simple reason that they were on break for the holidays. In fact, many economists who explicitly factored regional data into their models had lower forecasts than the consensus. Morgan Stanley had 55.6, for example.</p>, <p>That doesn\u2019t make the drop in the manufacturing index any better, but it is hard know just how much to worry. First, 54.1 is still well above the level of 50 that marks the dividing line between manufacturing expansion and contraction. Second, like most survey-based measures, the ISM index doesn\u2019t merely reflect the reality on the ground but also the mood of respondents.</p>, <p>And what might their mood have been in December, when worries about trade were front and center and stocks were plunging? Lousy\u2014but not as lousy as in 1998 when, in response to the financial-market convulsions from the Russian debt crisis, the index fell from 49.3 in August to 46.8 in December. The next year, 1999, the economy grew at its fastest pace in 15 years, a level not seen since.</p>, <p>While there are enough problems looming to expect the economy will, in fact, slow down this year, December\u2019s drop probably isn\u2019t nearly as scary as it seems.</p>, <p class="summary">The drop in the ISM index may have as much to do with manufacturers\u2019 mood as with what is happening on the factory floor </p>, <p class="hide" id="email-scrim-error">An error has occurred, please try again later.</p>, <p class="ty-headline">Thank you</p>, <p class="ty-message">This article has been sent to</p>, <p class="ty-recipient"></p>, <p class="style__column-name_2q_SeZUL5gpEK9-5nObB5R ">WSJ Membership</p>, <p class="style__column-name_2q_SeZUL5gpEK9-5nObB5R ">Customer Service</p>, <p class="style__column-name_2q_SeZUL5gpEK9-5nObB5R ">Tools &amp; Features</p>, <p class="style__column-name_2q_SeZUL5gpEK9-5nObB5R ">Ads</p>, <p class="style__column-name_2q_SeZUL5gpEK9-5nObB5R ">More</p>, <p class="print-copyright"></p>, <p class="print-copy"></p>]
